\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.
\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

She can choose x_n=0 to keep Q_n unchanged, which might allow Bazza to later increase S_n more. So perhaps she should choose positive x_n to increase Q_n, thereby reducing the remaining "slack" for squares. However, her own constraint S_n ≤ λ n must be satisfied; if she chooses large x_n, S_n increases, and she must ensure it doesn't exceed λ n. Since she can choose any x_n, she can pick x_n such that S_n is exactly λ n (or less). So she can push S_n up to the bound. That might be beneficial to restrict Bazza? Because if S_n is large, then for the next even turn (n+1), Bazza will have S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1}, and he wants to make S_{n+1} > λ (n+1) for the next Alice turn? Actually Bazza's goal is to make S_{n} > λ (n+1) for some even n? Let's define precisely.

Turn n:
- If n odd (Alice): she chooses x_n such that S_n ≤ λ n.
- If n even (Bazza): he chooses x_n such that Q_n ≤ n.

Bazza's win condition: at some odd turn, Alice cannot move. That happens if before her move, S_{n-1} > λ n. Since n is odd, n-1 is even. So Bazza wins if he can make S_{n-1} > λ n for some even n-1. He controls x_{n-1} on turn n-1 (even). So he can try to make S_{n-1} large enough.

Thus, the game can be thought of as: For each even turn n, Bazza chooses x_n, which adds to S_n (since S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n). He can make S_n as large as possible given his constraint on Q_n. But he also has to consider that Alice will then on turn n+1 (odd) choose x_{n+1} such that S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n is already > λ (n+1), then Alice cannot move, so Bazza wins immediately. So Bazza's aim is to push S_n above λ (n+1). If he can achieve that for some n, he wins.

Alice's aim is to keep S_n ≤ λ n on her turns, and also ensure that after her moves, the sum S_n is not too large to allow Bazza to exceed λ (n+1) on his next turn. She can choose x_n to control S_n. She might try to keep S_n as low as possible to prevent Bazza from making a big jump. But she also must satisfy the cumulative sum constraint S_n ≤ λ n. She can always choose x_n=0 to keep S_n = S_{n-1}. However, if she does that, then S_n = S_{n-1} (from previous even turn). If that S_{n-1} is already > λ n? That can't happen because after Bazza's move at n-1 (even), S_{n-1} could be > λ n? Possibly, if Bazza made S_{n-1} large, then Alice would lose on her turn at n. So Bazza would win if he can make S_{n-1} > λ n. So Alice must prevent that. Therefore, she wants to ensure that after her moves, S_n is not too large, so that Bazza cannot later push it over λ (n+1). But she also can influence the squares budget.

This is reminiscent of a "game" where each player can control a resource that the other uses. It might be solved by considering the "worst-case" sequence that allows the game to continue indefinitely. This is reminiscent of a "balancing" condition: he wants to make (2λ - a_k) as small as possible (to avoid large squares in the next step) while also keeping d_k positive. We can analyze the recurrence more systematically. We want to find the set of λ for which there exists a sequence (a_k) such that for all k, d_k ≥ 0 and a_k ∈ [0, 2λ]. The recurrence:

d_1 = 2 - (λ^2 + a_1^2) ≥ 0 ⇒ a_1^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2.
For k≥2: d_k = d_{k-1} + 2 - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 - a_k^2 ≥ 0.

But d_{k-1} is the deficit from the previous step. So essentially, the sum of squares of the two increments (the previous Alice's increment and the current Bazza's move) is bounded by 2 plus the previous deficit.

Let's think more systematically. Bazza wants to force that at some even turn, his chosen a_k > 2λ. He can choose his moves sequentially. Alice will always set her x_n to the maximum allowed given the sum from previous moves, because she wants to survive. So the process is deterministic given Bazza's choices. The state after Alice's turn at odd n=2k-1 is S_{2k-1}= (2k-1)λ, and Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2, where a_{k-1} = x_{2(k-1)}. Then Bazza chooses a_k, which must satisfy Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2 ≤ 2k. So a_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. Then the next Alice move is x_{2k+1} = 2λ - a_k. So the recurrence is:

Given a_0=0 (no previous Bazza move), and for k≥1, define:
S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ,
Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2,
where Q_{2k-2} = q_{k-1} (the square sum after Bazza's move at n=2k-2). And we have q_{k-1} = Q_{2k-2} (after Bazza's move). Then we choose a_k such that a_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. Then we set q_k = Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2. Then the next Alice increment is (2λ - a_k). So the process is:

Initialize q_0 = 0.
For k=1,2,...:
  Let d_k = 2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2? Wait, we have Q_{2k-1} = q_{k-1} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2. Then the maximum a_k is sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) = sqrt(2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2). So we have:

a_k ≤ sqrt(2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2). Then q_k = q_{k-1} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 + a_k^2.

Note that q_k ≤ 2k by construction. The process is a kind of "budget" where Bazza can decide to use some of his budget to increase the sum S. The question is: can Bazza force that at some step, a_k > 2λ? That is, can he choose his a_k such that this inequality holds? He can choose a_k less than the maximum, but to get a_k > 2λ, he needs that the maximum allowed a_k is > 2λ. So he needs sqrt(2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2) > 2λ. This is equivalent to 2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 > 4λ^2, i.e., q_{k-1} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 < 2k - 4λ^2. Since q_{k-1} ≤ 2(k-1) (by previous step), the right side is 2k - 4λ^2. For large k, the right side is about 2k, while q_{k-1} is about 2(k-1) if he uses full budget, so the inequality becomes 2(k-1) + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 < 2k - 4λ^2 ⇒ (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 < 2 - 4λ^2 ⇒ |2λ - a_{k-1}| < sqrt(2-4λ^2). So we have a recurrence. The condition for a_3 > 2λ is that there exists a_1, a_2 such that 6 - q_3 - (2λ - a_2)^2 > 4λ^2. This is 6 - q_3 - (2λ - a_2)^2 > 4λ^2 ⇒ q_3 + (2λ - a_2)^2 < 6 - 4λ^2. Since q_3 = λ^2 + a_1^2 + (2λ - a_1)^2 + a_2^2 = 5λ^2 -4λ a_1 + 2a_1^2 + a_2^2. So the condition becomes 5λ^2 -4λ a_1 + 2a_1^2 + a_2^2 + (2λ - a_2)^2 < 6 - 4λ^2 ⇒ 5λ^2 -4λ a_1 + 2a_1^2 + a_2^2 + 4λ^2 -4λ a_2 + a_2^2 < 6 - 4λ^2 ⇒ 9λ^2 -4λ a_1 -4λ a_2 + 2a_1^2 + 2a_2^2 < 6 - 4λ^2 ⇒ 13λ^2 -4λ a_1 -4λ a_2 + 2a_1^2 + 2a_2^2 < 6 ⇒ 2a_1^2 + 2a_2^2 -4λ a_1 -4λ a_2 + 13λ^2 -6 < 0. This is a quadratic form. The minimum of the left side over a_1, a_2 can be found by completing squares. It is a quadratic in a_1, a_2: 2(a_1^2 -2λ a_1) + 2(a_2^2 -2λ a_2) + 13λ^2 -6 = 2[(a_1-λ)^2 - λ^2] + 2[(a_2-λ)^2 - λ^2] + 13λ^2 -6 = 2(a_1-λ)^2 + 2(a_2-λ)^2 -2λ^2 -2λ^2 +13λ^2 -6 = 2(a_1-λ)^2 + 2(a_2-λ)^2 +9λ^2 -6. This strategy works because Alice will always set her x_n = λ (max) each turn, given that the sum S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ. This is consistent. So for any λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy. This is a typical "resource" game: Alice has a linear budget, Bazza has a quadratic budget, and they alternate. For λ>1, the quadratic budget is "tighter" relative to the linear growth, so Bazza will run out of squares eventually. We need to prove that for λ>1, Alice can force a win. In general, for λ>1, the recurrence will eventually lead to a step where the radicand becomes negative. This can be proved by showing that if Bazza always uses the maximum budget, then the quantity a_k converges to something less than 2λ - √2? Actually we can show that after some steps, the condition a_k ≥ 2λ - √2 cannot be maintained because the square budget will become insufficient. The key is that the sum of squares Q_n grows at least quadratically in n, while the constraint is linear. More formally, we can prove that for λ>1, there exists a constant C such that after a finite number of moves, the deficit d_k becomes negative. This is typical in such games: the linear budget for Bazza is eventually exhausted. We can use the invariant that S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. Since Alice sets S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ, we have Q_{2k-1} ≥ ((2k-1)λ)^2 / (2k-1) = (2k-1)λ^2. So Q_{2k-1} ≥ (2k-1)λ^2. Since λ^2 > 1, this is > (2k-1). But Bazza's constraint at turn 2k is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. However, Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2. So we need (2k-1)λ^2 + a_k^2 ≤ 2k. This implies a_k^2 ≤ 2k - (2k-1)λ^2. For large k, the right side becomes negative because λ^2 > 1, so for k large enough, 2k - (2k-1)λ^2 < 0. Therefore, there exists some k such that this inequality cannot hold, regardless of Bazza's choices. But wait, the inequality Q_{2k-1} ≥ (2k-1)λ^2 is a lower bound; it is not necessarily achieved if Bazza chooses a_k not maximal? But we are assuming Alice always plays max, so S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ. Then by Cauchy-Schwarz, Q_{2k-1} ≥ S_{2k-1}^2 / (2k-1) = (2k-1)λ^2. So indeed, Q_{2k-1} ≥ (2k-1)λ^2. This is a lower bound that holds for any sequence of numbers. So no matter what Bazza does, after Alice's odd turn at n=2k-1, the square sum is at least (2k-1)λ^2. Then for Bazza's next even turn at n=2k, we have Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2 ≥ (2k-1)λ^2 + a_k^2. The constraint is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. So we must have (2k-1)λ^2 + a_k^2 ≤ 2k. Thus a_k^2 ≤ 2k - (2k-1)λ^2. For large k, the right side is negative if λ^2 > 1, because 2k - (2k-1)λ^2 = 2k - 2kλ^2 + λ^2 = λ^2 - 2k(λ^2-1). Since λ^2-1 > 0, for k > λ^2/(2(λ^2-1)), the right side becomes negative. Therefore, there exists some k such that it's impossible for Bazza to choose any a_k ≥ 0 satisfying the inequality, because the required a_k^2 would have to be nonnegative and also ≤ a negative number. Hence, Bazza will have no legal move at his turn 2k, meaning Alice wins. This proof does not rely on any specific strategy of Bazza; it shows that if Alice always plays x_n = λ (max), then eventually, after some odd turn, the square sum will be so large that Bazza cannot make a move. More precisely, Alice's strategy ensures that after each of her turns, the square sum Q_{2k-1} is at least (2k-1)λ^2. Since λ^2 > 1, for sufficiently large k, we have (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k, because (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k ⇔ λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) ≈ 1, which holds for λ>1. Actually we need to show that there exists a k such that (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k, i.e., λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1). Since the right side approaches 1 from above, for λ>1, we can always choose k large enough such that 2k/(2k-1) < λ^2. This is true because 2k/(2k-1) = 1 + 1/(2k-1) → 1, and λ^2 > 1, so there exists K such that for all k ≥ K, 1 + 1/(2k-1) < λ^2. Then for k=K, we have (2K-1)λ^2 > 2K. Then the inequality (2K-1)λ^2 + a_K^2 ≤ 2K would require a_K^2 ≤ 2K - (2K-1)λ^2 < 0, impossible. Thus Bazza cannot survive at his K-th even turn. So Alice wins.

Therefore, for any λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy (by always setting her numbers to λ). For λ = 1, this strategy leads to Q_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ^2 = 2k-1, and then the constraint for Bazza at turn 2k is Q_{2k} ≤ 2k, so we need (2k-1) + a_k^2 ≤ 2k ⇒ a_k^2 ≤ 1, which is possible (e.g., a_k=1). So he can survive indefinitely, leading to a draw. So at λ=1, neither wins.

For λ < 1, we need to show that Bazza has a winning strategy. We already have a strategy: Choose a_1 = λ, a_2 = λ, ..., a_k = λ for some large k such that sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ. Then at his (k+1)th move, set a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, causing Alice to lose at her next turn. This strategy is feasible because the square sum after each of his moves q_k = 2kλ^2 ≤ 2k (since λ^2 ≤ 1). So he survives. So for any λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy. At λ=1/√2? Actually for λ=1/√2, the strategy would require some k such that sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)*1/2) = sqrt(2k+2 - k - 0.5) = sqrt(k+1.5) > 2λ = √2. For k large, sqrt(k+1.5) > √2, so win. So λ=1/√2 is also a win for Bazza. So the winning set for Bazza is λ < 1. However, we also have the possibility that for λ in (0,1), the strategy a_i = λ works for some k. So yes, Bazza wins for all λ < 1.

Now, we must also consider λ=1 exactly: we already have a draw. So the classification is:

- Alice wins iff λ > 1.
- Bazza wins iff 0 < λ < 1.
- At λ = 1, the game can go on forever (draw).

But wait, we need to check if for λ in (0,1), Bazza's strategy is indeed a winning strategy. Let's prove more rigorously: For λ < 1, define a_1 = λ, a_2 = λ, ..., a_k = λ for some k. The feasibility: At step 1, we have a_1 = λ. The square sum after his first move is q_1 = λ^2 + λ^2 = 2λ^2 ≤ 2 (since λ^2 ≤ 1). So okay. At each step i, we have q_i = 2iλ^2, and the condition for his next move is that the square sum after Alice's move at turn 2i+1 is Q_{2i+1} = q_i + (2λ - a_i)^2 = 2iλ^2 + λ^2 = (2i+1)λ^2. Then he can choose a_{i+1} = λ (which is ≤ sqrt(2(i+1) - Q_{2i+1}) = sqrt(2i+2 - (2i+1)λ^2). Since λ^2 ≤ 1, we need to check that λ ≤ sqrt(2i+2 - (2i+1)λ^2). Square both sides: λ^2 ≤ 2i+2 - (2i+1)λ^2 ⇒ (2i+2)λ^2 ≤ 2i+2 ⇒ λ^2 ≤ 1, which holds. So a_{i+1}=λ is feasible. So he can continue this strategy indefinitely. Then after k steps, we have q_k = 2kλ^2. At his (k+1)th move (turn 2k+2), we have Q_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ^2. He then chooses a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ. This is possible if the radicand is positive, which is true for all k because λ^2 < 1, so 2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2 > 2k+2 - (2k+1) = 1 > 0. So he can set a_{k+1} = that value. Then after his move, Q_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ^2 + (2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) = 2k+2, so the constraint holds. Then Alice at her next turn (n=2k+3) must choose x_{2k+3} = 2λ - a_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2). Since we have chosen k such that sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, this x_{2k+3} is negative, which is not allowed. Therefore, Alice cannot move, and Bazza wins. So for any λ < 1, Bazza can choose k sufficiently large such that sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ. This condition is equivalent to 2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2 > 4λ^2 ⇒ 2k+2 > (2k+5)λ^2 ⇒ λ^2 < (2k+2)/(2k+5). Since the right side tends to 1 as k→∞, for any fixed λ^2 < 1, we can choose k such that this holds. For example, take k = ceil(3/(2(1-λ^2))) (since we need (2k+2)/(2k+5) > λ^2, which is equivalent to k > 3/(2(1-λ^2))). So Bazza has a winning strategy.

Thus, the answer is: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bazza wins for 0 < λ < 1, and at λ = 1, neither wins (the game can go on forever). So the sets are:

- Alice has a winning strategy iff λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff 0 < λ < 1.

Now, the problem statement: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we should output:

Alice wins: λ > 1.
Bazza wins: 0 < λ < 1.

But we should also note that at λ = 1, the game can go on forever, so neither has a winning strategy. The problem asks for those for which Alice has a winning strategy, and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. So we can just give the intervals.

However, we need to be careful: The game is defined for positive real number λ. So λ can be any positive real. The analysis above shows that for λ in (0,1), Bazza wins; for λ>1, Alice wins; for λ=1, draw. So the answer is: Alice wins if and only if λ > 1; Bazza wins if and only if 0 < λ < 1.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. The proof should be self-contained, with careful induction and inequalities.

Let's structure the proof:

First, define the game. We'll denote by (x_n) the chosen numbers. The game continues indefinitely if for all n, there exists a legal choice. The players choose numbers adaptively.

We'll prove:

Lemma 1: For λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.
Proof: Alice's strategy: On each of her turns (odd n), she chooses x_n = λ. We show that after some finite number of turns, Bazza cannot move. Suppose the game reaches turn n = 2k-1 (odd) and Alice has chosen x_1 = λ, x_3 = λ, ..., x_{2k-1} = λ. Then the sum S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ. By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, we have (S_{2k-1})^2 ≤ (2k-1) Q_{2k-1}, so Q_{2k-1} ≥ (S_{2k-1})^2 / (2k-1) = (2k-1)λ^2. Since λ > 1, we have (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k for sufficiently large k. In fact, for k > (λ^2)/(2(λ^2-1)), we have (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k. Let k0 be the smallest integer such that (2k0-1)λ^2 > 2k0. Then after Alice's turn at n = 2k0-1, we have Q_{2k0-1} ≥ (2k0-1)λ^2 > 2k0. Then Bazza's next turn is at n = 2k0 (even), and he must choose x_{2k0} such that Q_{2k0} = Q_{2k0-1} + x_{2k0}^2 ≤ 2k0. Since Q_{2k0-1} > 2k0, this is impossible because x_{2k0}^2 ≥ 0. Therefore, Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins. This proves that for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.

Note: The existence of such k0 is guaranteed because λ^2 > 1, so (2k-1)λ^2 grows faster than 2k. More explicitly, the condition (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k is equivalent to λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) = 1 + 1/(2k-1). Since the right side approaches 1, for any λ^2 > 1 there exists k such that this holds. So the strategy works.

Lemma 2: For λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy.
Proof: Bazza's strategy: On each of his turns (even n), he will choose x_n = λ (the same as Alice's value) for the first k turns, where k is a positive integer chosen large enough so that after his k-th move, the condition for the next move is satisfied. More precisely, we show that there exists a positive integer k such that if Bazza sets x_2 = x_4 = ... = x_{2k} = λ, then at his (k+1)-th move (turn 2k+2), he can choose a number a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, and then Alice will be unable to move at her next turn. We need to verify that this strategy is feasible: At step 1, Alice chooses x_1 = λ (max). Then Bazza chooses x_2 = λ. Then the cumulative sum after turn 2 is S_2 = 2λ, Q_2 = 2λ^2 ≤ 2 (since λ^2 ≤ 1). Alice at turn 3 chooses x_3 = 3λ - S_2 = λ. So S_3 = 3λ, Q_3 = 3λ^2. Continue inductively: After i steps, we have S_{2i-1} = (2i-1)λ, Q_{2i-1} = (2i-1)λ^2. Then Bazza chooses x_{2i} = λ. Then S_{2i} = (2i-1)λ + λ = 2iλ, Q_{2i} = (2i-1)λ^2 + λ^2 = 2iλ^2. Since λ^2 ≤ 1, we have Q_{2i} ≤ 2i, so the constraint is satisfied. This process can continue indefinitely. Now, choose k to be a positive integer such that λ^2 < (2k+2)/(2k+5). Since the right side tends to 1 as k→∞, such k exists. Then define a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2). We claim that a_{k+1} > 2λ. Indeed, squaring both sides gives (2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 4λ^2 ⇔ 2k+2 > (2k+5)λ^2 ⇔ λ^2 < (2k+2)/(2k+5), which holds by our choice of k. So Bazza can choose x_{2k+2} = a_{k+1}. Then after his move, we have Q_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ^2 + (2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) = 2k+2, satisfying the constraint. Then Alice's next turn is at n = 2k+3, and she must choose x_{2k+3} such that S_{2k+3} ≤ (2k+3)λ. But S_{2k+2} = 2kλ + a_{k+1} = 2kλ + sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2). Since a_{k+1} > 2λ, we have S_{2k+2} > 2kλ + 2λ = (2k+2)λ. Actually we need to compute S_{2k+2} precisely: S_{2k+2} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} + a_{k+1} = (2k-1)λ + λ + a_{k+1} = 2kλ + a_{k+1}. And we have a_{k+1} > 2λ, so S_{2k+2} > 2kλ + 2λ = (2k+2)λ. Then x_{2k+3} = (2k+3)λ - S_{2k+2} < (2k+3)λ - (2k+2)λ = λ. So x_{2k+3} < λ, but could it be negative? Actually we need to check if S_{2k+2} could be greater than (2k+3)λ, which would make x_{2k+3} negative. Since a_{k+1} > 2λ, we have S_{2k+2} > 2kλ + 2λ = (2k+2)λ. Also, (2k+3)λ = (2k+2)λ + λ. So S_{2k+2} could be less than (2k+3)λ if a_{k+1} < λ? But a_{k+1} > 2λ, so S_{2k+2} > (2k+2)λ + 2λ? Actually we have S_{2k+2} = 2kλ + a_{k+1}. Since a_{k+1} > 2λ, we have S_{2k+2} > 2kλ + 2λ = (2k+2)λ. And (2k+3)λ = (2k+2)λ + λ. So S_{2k+2} could be less than (2k+3)λ if a_{k+1} < λ, but a_{k+1} > 2λ, so it's > λ, so S_{2k+2} > (2k+2)λ + λ? Wait, no: a_{k+1} > 2λ, so S_{2k+2} > 2kλ + 2λ = (2k+2)λ. That is, S_{2k+2} > (2k+2)λ. But (2k+3)λ = (2k+2)λ + λ. So S_{2k+2} could be, say, (2k+2)λ + 0.1λ if a_{k+1}=2λ+0.1λ, then S_{2k+2} = 2kλ + (2λ+0.1λ) = (2k+2)λ + 0.1λ. That's less than (2k+3)λ = (2k+2)λ + λ, since 0.1λ < λ. So S_{2k+2} could be less than (2k+3)λ. Then x_{2k+3} = (2k+3)λ - S_{2k+2} > 0. So Alice can move. But we need x_{2k+3} to be nonnegative; it is. So the win condition is that x_{2k+3} is negative. That requires S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)λ. So we need a_{k+1} > λ? Actually S_{2k+2} = 2kλ + a_{k+1}. For this to be > (2k+3)λ, we need a_{k+1} > 3λ. But we have a_{k+1} > 2λ, but not necessarily > 3λ. So we need to ensure that the chosen k gives a_{k+1} > 3λ? That seems unlikely. And S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ (if Alice always sets her moves to max). Then the condition becomes (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1} > (2k+3)λ ⇒ a_{k+1} > 2λ. So indeed, a_{k+1} > 2λ is sufficient for Alice's loss. That's what we used. So we don't need a_{k+1} > 3λ. The condition is a_{k+1} > 2λ. So if we set a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, then S_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1} > (2k+1)λ + 2λ = (2k+3)λ, so Alice cannot move. Good.

Thus, the strategy works.

Now, we must also consider λ=1. For λ=1, Alice's strategy (always set x_n = λ) leads to Q_{2k-1} = 2k-1, and Bazza can survive by setting a_k = 1 each time, leading to infinite play. So neither has a winning strategy. So the answer is as above.

We analyze the two-player game with a fixed \(\lambda>0\).  
Turn \(n\) (starting with \(n=1\)):

- If \(n\) is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real \(x_n\) such that  
  \[
  \sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i \le \lambda n .
  \]
- If \(n\) is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real \(x_n\) such that  
  \[
  \sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i^2 \le n .
  \]

If a player cannot make a legal move, the other player wins.  
If the game continues indefinitely, neither wins.

We determine for which \(\lambda\) Alice (resp. Bazza) has a winning strategy.

**Alice wins for \(\lambda>1\).**  
Alice’s strategy: on every odd turn she chooses \(x_n = \lambda\).  
Suppose the game reaches an odd turn \(n=2k-1\) and that Alice has chosen \(x_1=\lambda\), \(x_3=\lambda,\dots,x_{2k-1}=\lambda\).  
Then the cumulative sum is  
\[
S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)\lambda .
\]  
By the Cauchy–Schwarz inequality,  
\[
S_{2k-1}^2 \le (2k-1) Q_{2k-1},
\]  
where \(Q_{2k-1} = \sum_{i=1}^{2k-1} x_i^2\). Hence  
\[
Q_{2k-1} \ge \frac{S_{2k-1}^2}{2k-1} = (2k-1)\lambda^2 .
\]  
Because \(\lambda>1\) we have \(\lambda^2>1\). The inequality  
\[
(2k-1)\lambda^2 > 2k
\]  
holds for all sufficiently large \(k\) (since the right‑hand side \(\frac{2k}{2k-1} \to 1\)).  
Let \(k_0\) be the smallest integer for which \((2k_0-1)\lambda^2 > 2k_0\).  
After Alice’s turn at \(n=2k_0-1\) we have \(Q_{2k_0-1} \ge (2k_0-1)\lambda^2 > 2k_0\).  

Bazza’s next turn is at \(n=2k_0\) (even). He must choose \(x_{2k_0}\) so that  
\[
Q_{2k_0} = Q_{2k_0-1} + x_{2k_0}^2 \le 2k_0 .
\]  
But \(Q_{2k_0-1} > 2k_0\) and \(x_{2k_0}^2 \ge 0\) imply \(Q_{2k_0} > 2k_0\), which is impossible.  
Thus Bazza has no legal move, Alice wins.  
Hence Alice has a winning strategy for every \(\lambda>1\).

**Bazza wins for \(0<\lambda<1\).**  
Bazza’s strategy: on his first \(k\) even turns he chooses \(x_n = \lambda\).  
We show that a suitable \(k\) exists so that after his \(k\)-th move he can force Alice’s next move to be impossible.  
Inductively, if Bazza sets \(x_2 = \lambda\), \(x_4 = \lambda,\dots,x_{2k} = \lambda\) and Alice always plays the maximum allowed (which is \(\lambda\) on each of her turns), then  
\[
S_{2i-1} = (2i-1)\lambda,\qquad Q_{2i-1} = (2i-1)\lambda^2,\qquad 
S_{2i} = 2i\lambda,\qquad Q_{2i} = 2i\lambda^2 \le 2i .
\]  
Thus the constraints are satisfied for any \(k\).  

Now choose a positive integer \(k\) such that  
\[
\lambda^2 < \frac{2k+2}{2k+5}.
\]  
Such \(k\) exists because the right‑hand side tends to \(1\) as \(k\to\infty\) and \(\lambda^2<1\).  
On his next even turn (turn \(2k+2\)) Bazza chooses  
\[
a_{k+1} = \sqrt{2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2}.
\]  
We have \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\) because squaring gives  
\[
2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2 > 4\lambda^2
\iff 2k+2 > (2k+5)\lambda^2
\iff \lambda^2 < \frac{2k+2}{2k+5},
\]  
which holds by our choice of \(k\).  

After this move,  
\[
Q_{2k+2} = (2k+1)\lambda^2 + \bigl(2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2\bigr) = 2k+2,
\]  
so Bazza’s constraint is satisfied.  
Alice’s next turn is at \(n=2k+3\). The sum before her move is  
\[
S_{2k+2} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} + a_{k+1} = (2k-1)\lambda + \lambda + a_{k+1} = 2k\lambda + a_{k+1}.
\]  
Since \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\), we obtain  
\[
S_{2k+2} > 2k\lambda + 2\lambda = (2k+2)\lambda.
\]  
The linear condition for Alice at \(n=2k+3\) requires  
\[
S_{2k+2} \le \lambda (2k+3) = (2k+2)\lambda + \lambda.
\]  
But \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+2)\lambda\), and actually the inequality \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\) would force \(x_{2k+3}\) to be negative.  
Because \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\), we have  
\[
S_{2k+2} = 2k\lambda + a_{k+1} > 2k\lambda + 2\lambda = (2k+2)\lambda.
\]  
However, we need the stronger bound \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\) to make Alice’s move impossible.  
Is \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\)?  
We have \(S_{2k+2} = 2k\lambda + a_{k+1}\).  
The inequality \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\) gives \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+2)\lambda\).  
But \((2k+3)\lambda = (2k+2)\lambda + \lambda\).  
Thus \(S_{2k+2}\) could be less than \((2k+3)\lambda\) if \(a_{k+1} < \lambda\).  
We need \(a_{k+1} > 3\lambda\) for the condition \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\).  
But we only have \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\).  
So is the win condition satisfied?  

Let's recalc: Alice's turn at \(n=2k+3\) requires \(S_{2k+2} \le (2k+3)\lambda\).  
If \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\), then she cannot move.  
We have \(S_{2k+2} = 2k\lambda + a_{k+1}\).  
So we need \(2k\lambda + a_{k+1} > (2k+3)\lambda \iff a_{k+1} > 3\lambda\).  
Thus we need \(a_{k+1} > 3\lambda\).  

But our earlier condition for winning at step \(k+1\) was that \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\)?  
Let's double-check the earlier derivation: In the "maximal" strategy for Bazza, we had \(a_k = \sqrt{2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2}\). For him to win, we need that Alice's next move is negative, i.e., \(S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)\lambda\).  
We have \(S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + a_{k+1}\).  
If Alice always plays the maximum, then \(S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)\lambda\).  
So the condition is \((2k+1)\lambda + a_{k+1} > (2k+3)\lambda \iff a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\).  
That's what we had earlier. But wait: (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1} > (2k+3)λ simplifies to a_{k+1} > 2λ, not 3λ.  
I mis-calculated: (2k+3)λ = (2k+1)λ + 2λ. So indeed, we need a_{k+1} > 2λ.  
But S_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1}? No, S_{2k+2} is the sum after Bazza's move at turn 2k+2. The state before Bazza's move at turn 2k+2 is after Alice's move at turn 2k+1, which is S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ. Then Bazza chooses a_{k+1} = x_{2k+2}, so S_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1}. That's correct. Then Alice's next turn is at n=2k+3, and she needs S_{2k+2} ≤ (2k+3)λ. If a_{k+1} > 2λ, then S_{2k+2} > (2k+1)λ + 2λ = (2k+3)λ, so she cannot move. So the condition is a_{k+1} > 2λ. That's correct. So we don't need a_{k+1} > 3λ. My earlier calculation of S_{2k+2} = 2kλ + a_{k+1} was wrong because I used S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ, not 2kλ. Let's recompute: In the strategy, after k steps, we have S_{2k} = 2kλ? Actually with Bazza's strategy a_i = λ for i=1..k, and Alice's strategy x_{2i-1}=λ, we have after turn 2k (even), the sum S_{2k} = 2kλ? Because S_2 = λ+λ=2λ, S_4 = 3λ+λ=4λ? Wait, we need to be precise: At turn 1, Alice sets x_1=λ, so S_1=λ. Then Bazza sets x_2=λ, so S_2=2λ. Then Alice sets x_3=3λ - S_2 = λ, so S_3=3λ. Then Bazza sets x_4=λ, so S_4=4λ. So indeed, after each even turn, S_{2k}=2kλ. After each odd turn, S_{2k-1}=(2k-1)λ. So the state before Bazza's (k+1)th move (turn 2k+2) is after Alice's turn at n=2k+1, which gives S_{2k+1}=(2k+1)λ. Then Bazza chooses a_{k+1}=x_{2k+2}, so S_{2k+2} = (2k+1)λ + a_{k+1}. That's the correct expression. So we need a_{k+1} > 2λ to cause loss. That's the condition we already have.

Thus, the strategy works: choose k such that sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, which is equivalent to λ^2 < (2k+2)/(2k+5). This is possible because the right side approaches 1. So Bazza can win.

The game is complex, but we can use a "potential" argument. I recall a classic problem: Alice and Bazza choose numbers with constraints on sum and sum of squares. The critical value is λ=1. We can treat the game as a "resource" allocation: At each turn, the player can decide to spend his resource to increase the sum. The constraints are:

Perhaps the key is that the outcome is determined by the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. If Alice plays to maximize S_n (by setting x_n = λ), then Q_{2k-1} ≥ (2k-1)λ^2. The problem likely has a known solution: Alice wins iff λ > 1, Bazza wins iff λ < 1, and at λ = 1 it's a draw. We need to solve the game. The game is played with a fixed λ>0. Turns n=1,2,3,...:

- Odd n: Alice chooses nonnegative real x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n.
- Even n: Bazza chooses nonnegative real x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the other wins. If game goes forever, neither wins.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: The players choose numbers sequentially, with constraints on cumulative sums (for Alice's turns) and cumulative sum of squares (for Bazza's turns). The game ends when a player cannot make a legal move; the other player wins. So each player wants to force the opponent into a position where no legal move exists.

We need to analyze the game as a kind of "pursuit" or "resource" game. The constraints are cumulative, so the game is about controlling the partial sums.

Let’s denote:

For n odd: after Alice's move, the sum S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n.
For n even: after Bazza's move, the sum of squares Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

The players are not required to maximize anything; they can choose any nonnegative real numbers satisfying the constraint.

The game continues indefinitely if both players can always make a move.

We need to determine the "critical" λ where the outcome changes.

Observation: The constraints are linear in the sum for Alice, and quadratic for Bazza. The players choose numbers; the constraints are on the cumulative sums. So essentially, the state can be described by the current cumulative sum (for Alice) and cumulative sum of squares (for Bazza). But note that the constraints are not symmetric: Alice's constraint depends only on the sum (linear), Bazza's depends only on the sum of squares (quadratic). Moreover, Alice moves on odd turns, Bazza on even turns.

We need to consider strategies. Possibly, the game is similar to a "bounded sum" game where each player tries to keep the partial sums within certain bounds.

Let's analyze the dynamic more generally.

The game can be viewed as: At each even turn, Bazza chooses x_n, which adds to S_n and Q_n. He wants to ensure that for all odd n, Alice can move (i.e., S_{n-1} ≤ λ n) and also that he can move (i.e., Q_n ≤ n). He can also try to make the game go on forever. So we need to find for which λ Bazza has a strategy to avoid losing indefinitely, i.e., to force the game to go on forever. And for which λ Alice has a strategy to force a win. The problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we need to partition the λ>0 into three sets: A (Alice wins), B (Bazza wins), and D (draw, neither wins). The draw set is where optimal play leads to infinite play.

From the analysis, we have:
- For λ<1, Bazza can force a win. We saw an example for λ=0.5, he can win quickly. But we need to prove that for any λ<1, Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly by making his moves large enough to eventually make S_{n-1} > λ n. Since λ<1, the linear bound is less than the index. He can use the squares constraint to increase S_n. For instance, he can aim to make S_{2k} = 2k? But if λ<1, then 2k > 2kλ? Actually he wants S_{2k} > λ(2k+1)? Not exactly. He wants to cause Alice to lose at some odd turn. Let's try to construct a winning strategy for Bazza for λ<1.

- For λ>1, Alice can force a win. We saw that for λ=1.1, she can win. But we need to check if for λ just above 1, say λ=1.01, she can still win. Our earlier attempt to follow the "balanced" strategy for Bazza failed for large k because the condition fails. But maybe Bazza has a different strategy to avoid loss? We need to find the set of λ for which Bazza can force an infinite play (i.e., he can ensure that for all n, a legal move exists). That is, he can keep the game going forever. This is equivalent to his ability to "respond" to Alice's moves in such a way that the cumulative constraints are never violated. Since Alice is trying to win, she will try to force a loss for Bazza. So we need to analyze the game as a "deterministic" game where both players choose numbers. We can consider the "critical" λ where the outcome changes.

But we also need that the move is possible: we must have q_k ≤ 2k, which is equivalent to d_k ≥ 0. So we want to choose a_k such that d_k is maximized, i.e., a_k = 0. But we saw that if we set a_2=0, then d_2 = 0.939, and then for k=3, we have d_3 = d_2 + 2 - (2λ - a_2)^2 - a_3^2 = 0.939 + 2 - (2.02)^2 - a_3^2 = 0.939 + 2 - 4.0804 - a_3^2 = -1.1414 - a_3^2, negative. So that's not good. So we need to choose a_k not too small to keep the next term manageable. So the optimal strategy for Bazza is to choose a_k to keep d_k nonnegative, and also to make the next step possible. This is reminiscent of a "balancing" condition: he wants to make (2λ - a_k) as small as possible (to avoid large squares in the next step) while also keeping d_k positive. Since d_k = d_{k-1} + 2 - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 - a_k^2, the term (2λ - a_k)^2 will appear in the next step. So there is a trade-off.

We can analyze the recurrence more systematically. We want to find the set of λ for which there exists a sequence (a_k) such that for all k, d_k ≥ 0 and a_k ∈ [0, 2λ]. The recurrence:

d_1 = 2 - (λ^2 + a_1^2) ≥ 0 ⇒ a_1^2 ≤ 2 - λ^2.
For k≥2: d_k = d_{k-1} + 2 - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 - a_k^2 ≥ 0.
Also, we can choose a_k to be anything within [0, 2λ]. Note that the recurrence can be rewritten as:

(2λ - a_k)^2 ≤ 2 + d_{k-1} - a_k^2? Not directly. Let's derive the condition for the next step: d_k ≥ 0 is equivalent to (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 + a_k^2 ≤ 2 + d_{k-1}. But d_{k-1} is the deficit from the previous step. So essentially, the sum of squares of the two increments (the previous Alice's increment and the current Bazza's move) is bounded by 2 plus the previous deficit.

Given the sequence, we can think of the "cumulative" sums. This is similar to a game where the total "resource" is the sum of squares. There might be a known result: The threshold λ0 is the positive root of some equation, and the outcome changes at λ0. For k≥2: d_k = d_{k-1} + 2 - (2λ - c)^2 - c^2.
For constant deficit, we need d_k = d (constant) for all k, so:

The condition for a_3 > 2λ would involve Q_5? Actually we can analyze the general recurrence. The process is similar to a "resource" where he can allocate his moves to increase S. The maximum possible a_k that he can achieve (by always using the maximum budget) is given by the fixed point of the recurrence a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2) with initial a_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2). For λ>1, this process fails because the radicand becomes negative. For λ≤1, the sequence a_k is increasing and converges to a* = λ + √(1-λ^2) (if λ≤1). The value a* is the supremum of a_k that he can achieve in the "maximal" strategy. So if a* > 2λ, then he can eventually achieve a_k > 2λ (by taking a_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2) and then the sequence will reach that limit, but the sequence might not exceed 2λ if a* > 2λ? Actually if a* > 2λ, then the sequence a_k will eventually become greater than 2λ, because it converges to a* from below, and a* > 2λ, so there exists some k such that a_k > 2λ. So for λ such that a* > 2λ, he can win by playing the maximal strategy. That condition is λ + √(1-λ^2) > 2λ ⇔ √(1-λ^2) > λ ⇔ 1-λ^2 > λ^2 ⇔ 2λ^2 < 1 ⇔ λ^2 < 1/2 ⇔ λ < 1/√2. So for λ < 1/√2, the maximal strategy yields a_k eventually > 2λ, so he wins. For λ = 1/√2, a* = 2λ, so the sequence approaches exactly 2λ from below, and it never exceeds 2λ. Let's think more systematically. Bazza wants to force that at some even turn, his chosen a_k > 2λ. He can choose his moves sequentially. Alice will always set her x_n to the maximum allowed given the sum from previous moves, because she wants to survive. So the process is deterministic given Bazza's choices. The state after Alice's turn at odd n=2k-1 is S_{2k-1}= (2k-1)λ, and Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2, where a_{k-1} = x_{2(k-1)}. Then Bazza chooses a_k, which must satisfy Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2 ≤ 2k. So a_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. Then the next Alice move is x_{2k+1} = 2λ - a_k. So the recurrence is:

Given a_0=0 (no previous Bazza move), and for k≥1, define:
S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ,
Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2,
where Q_{2k-2} = q_{k-1} (the square sum after Bazza's move at n=2k-2). And we have q_{k-1} = Q_{2k-2} (after Bazza's move). Then we choose a_k such that a_k^2 ≤ 2k - Q_{2k-1}. Then we set q_k = Q_{2k} = Q_{2k-1} + a_k^2. Then the next Alice increment is (2λ - a_k). So the process is:

Initialize q_0 = 0.
For k=1,2,...:
  Let d_k = 2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2? Wait, we have Q_{2k-1} = q_{k-1} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2. Then the maximum a_k is sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) = sqrt(2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2). So we have:

a_k ≤ sqrt(2k - q_{k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2). Then q_k = q_{k-1} + (2λ - a_{k-1})^2 + a_k^2.

Note that q_k ≤ 2k by construction. The process is a kind of "budget" where Bazza can decide to use some of his budget to increase the sum S. This is possible because the right side approaches 1 from below. So for λ in (0,1), there exists a k such that the condition holds. But we also need that the a_i we choose (which are λ for i=1,...,k) are feasible at each step. The feasibility condition is that at each step, the square sum after Bazza's move must be ≤ 2k, i.e., q_k ≤ 2k. With a_i = λ, we have q_k = Q_{2k} = 2kλ^2. This is equivalent to λ^2 < (2k+2)/(2k+5). For large k, (2k+2)/(2k+5) ≈ 1 - 3/(2k), so we need λ^2 < 1 - 3/(2k). Since λ^2 is fixed, we can choose k such that 3/(2k) < 1 - λ^2, i.e., k > 3/(2(1-λ^2)). So for any λ < 1, there exists a k satisfying this. Therefore, Bazza can win for any λ < 1 by choosing a_1=λ, a_2=λ,..., a_k=λ for some k large enough, and then at his (k+1)th move (even turn 2(k+1)), he chooses a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ, causing Alice to lose at her next turn. This strategy works because Alice will always set her x_n = λ (max) each turn, given that the sum S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ. This is consistent. So for any λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy. For λ = 1, this strategy would require a_i =1 for all i, but then a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)*1) = sqrt(1) =1, which is not >2λ=2. So no win. So at λ=1, it's a draw.

Now, what about λ > 1? For λ > 1, the strategy a_i = λ is not feasible because the square sum q_k = 2kλ^2 would exceed 2k if λ^2 >1. So Bazza cannot set a_i = λ. He must choose a_i smaller. The question is: can he force a win for λ > 1? The condition for a_k > 2λ would require that after the previous steps, the deficit is large enough. The general recurrence shows that if Bazza always chooses the maximum possible a_i, then the quantity q_i (the square sum after his move) is exactly 2i, and the process continues with a_i = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_{i-1})^2) etc. But for λ>1, this recurrence may not be possible because (2λ - a_{i-1})^2 may be large. Let's analyze the "maximal" strategy for λ>1. The process is: given a_{i-1} and q_{i-1}=2(i-1), we choose a_i as large as possible: a_i = sqrt(2i - q_{i-1} - (2λ - a_{i-1})^2). So a_i is real only if (2λ - a_{i-1})^2 ≤ 2. Since λ>1, 2λ >2, so this condition may fail if a_{i-1} is not close enough to 2λ. For λ=1.1, 2λ=2.2. For example, choose a_1=0.8, then (2.2-0.8)=1.4, square=1.96, a_2 = sqrt(2-1.96)= sqrt(0.04)=0.2. So a_2=0.2, which is less than 2λ. Then a_2=0.2, and we can continue. So for λ>1, the "maximal" strategy cannot continue indefinitely; eventually, the radicand becomes negative. So Bazza cannot survive indefinitely; Alice will win. But we need to prove that for any λ>1, Alice has a winning strategy. We already have a strategy for Alice: she can always set her x_n = λ (max) each turn. Then the game is as we described. The question is: can Bazza always avoid losing? The above suggests that for λ>1, the deficit d_k = 2k - q_k becomes negative eventually, meaning he loses. But we need to show that no matter what choices he makes, the game will end with Alice winning (i.e., Bazza cannot force an infinite play). This is a typical "resource" game: Alice has a linear budget, Bazza has a quadratic budget, and they alternate. For λ>1, the quadratic budget is "tighter" relative to the linear growth, so Bazza will run out of squares eventually. We need to prove that for λ>1, Alice can force a win. The simplest way: Show that if λ>1, then the "maximal" strategy for Bazza (i.e., always using the full budget) leads to a loss in finitely many steps. - Alice has a winning strategy iff λ > 1.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff 0 < λ < 1.

Now, the problem statement: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we should output:

Alice wins: λ > 1.
Bazza wins: 0 < λ < 1.

But we should also note that at λ = 1, the game can go on forever, so neither has a winning strategy. The problem asks for those for which Alice has a winning strategy, and those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. So we can just give the intervals.

However, we need to be careful: The game is defined for positive real number λ. So λ can be any positive real. The analysis above shows that for λ in (0,1), Bazza wins; for λ>1, Alice wins; for λ=1, draw. So the answer is: Alice wins if and only if λ > 1; Bazza wins if and only if 0 < λ < 1.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. The proof should be self-contained, with careful induction and inequalities.

Let's structure the proof:

First, define the game. We'll denote by (x_n) the chosen numbers. The game continues indefinitely if for all n, there exists a legal choice. The players choose numbers adaptively.

We'll prove:

Lemma 1: For λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.
Proof: Alice's strategy: On each of her turns (odd n), she chooses x_n = λ. We show that after some finite number of turns, Bazza cannot move. Suppose the game reaches turn n = 2k-1 (odd) and Alice has chosen x_1 = λ, x_3 = λ, ..., x_{2k-1} = λ. Then the sum S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)λ. By the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, we have (S_{2k-1})^2 ≤ (2k-1) Q_{2k-1}, so Q_{2k-1} ≥ (S_{2k-1})^2 / (2k-1) = (2k-1)λ^2. Since λ > 1, we have (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k for sufficiently large k. In fact, for k > (λ^2)/(2(λ^2-1)), we have (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k. Let k0 be the smallest integer such that (2k0-1)λ^2 > 2k0. Then after Alice's turn at n = 2k0-1, we have Q_{2k0-1} ≥ (2k0-1)λ^2 > 2k0. Then Bazza's next turn is at n = 2k0 (even), and he must choose x_{2k0} such that Q_{2k0} = Q_{2k0-1} + x_{2k0}^2 ≤ 2k0. Since Q_{2k0-1} > 2k0, this is impossible because x_{2k0}^2 ≥ 0. Therefore, Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins. This proves that for λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.

Note: The existence of such k0 is guaranteed because λ^2 > 1, so (2k-1)λ^2 grows faster than 2k. More explicitly, the condition (2k-1)λ^2 > 2k is equivalent to λ^2 > 2k/(2k-1) = 1 + 1/(2k-1). Since the right side approaches 1, for any λ^2 > 1 there exists k such that this holds. So the strategy works.

Lemma 2: For λ < 1, Bazza has a winning strategy.
Proof: Bazza's strategy: On each of his turns (even n), he will choose x_n = λ (the same as Alice's value) for the first k turns, where k is a positive integer chosen large enough so that after his k-th move, the condition for the next move is satisfied. Now, we must also consider λ=1. For λ=1, Alice's strategy (always set x_n = λ) leads to Q_{2k-1} = 2k-1, and Bazza can survive by setting a_k = 1 each time, leading to infinite play. So neither has a winning strategy. So the answer is as above.

We analyze the two-player game with a fixed \(\lambda>0\).  
Turn \(n\) (starting with \(n=1\)):

- If \(n\) is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real \(x_n\) such that  
  \[
  \sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i \le \lambda n .
  \]
- If \(n\) is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real \(x_n\) such that  
  \[
  \sum_{i=1}^{n} x_i^2 \le n .
  \]

If a player cannot make a legal move, the other player wins.  
If the game continues indefinitely, neither wins.

We determine for which \(\lambda\) Alice (resp. Bazza) has a winning strategy.

**Alice wins for \(\lambda>1\).**  
Alice’s strategy: on every odd turn she chooses \(x_n = \lambda\).  
Suppose the game reaches an odd turn \(n=2k-1\) and that Alice has chosen \(x_1=\lambda\), \(x_3=\lambda,\dots,x_{2k-1}=\lambda\).  
Then the cumulative sum is  
\[
S_{2k-1} = (2k-1)\lambda .
\]  
By the Cauchy–Schwarz inequality,  
\[
S_{2k-1}^2 \le (2k-1) Q_{2k-1},
\]  
where \(Q_{2k-1} = \sum_{i=1}^{2k-1} x_i^2\). Hence  
\[
Q_{2k-1} \ge \frac{S_{2k-1}^2}{2k-1} = (2k-1)\lambda^2 .
\]  
Because \(\lambda>1\) we have \(\lambda^2>1\). The inequality  
\[
(2k-1)\lambda^2 > 2k
\]  
holds for all sufficiently large \(k\) (since the right‑hand side \(\frac{2k}{2k-1} \to 1\)).  
Let \(k_0\) be the smallest integer for which \((2k_0-1)\lambda^2 > 2k_0\).  
After Alice’s turn at \(n=2k_0-1\) we have \(Q_{2k_0-1} \ge (2k_0-1)\lambda^2 > 2k_0\).  

Bazza’s next turn is at \(n=2k_0\) (even). He must choose \(x_{2k_0}\) so that  
\[
Q_{2k_0} = Q_{2k_0-1} + x_{2k_0}^2 \le 2k_0 .
\]  
But \(Q_{2k_0-1} > 2k_0\) and \(x_{2k_0}^2 \ge 0\) imply \(Q_{2k_0} > 2k_0\), which is impossible.  
Thus Bazza has no legal move, Alice wins.  
Hence Alice has a winning strategy for every \(\lambda>1\).

**Bazza wins for \(0<\lambda<1\).**  
Bazza’s strategy: on his first \(k\) even turns he chooses \(x_n = \lambda\).  
We show that a suitable \(k\) exists so that after his \(k\)-th move he can force Alice’s next move to be impossible.  
Inductively, if Bazza sets \(x_2 = \lambda\), \(x_4 = \lambda,\dots,x_{2k} = \lambda\) and Alice always plays the maximum allowed (which is \(\lambda\) on each of her turns), then  
\[
S_{2i-1} = (2i-1)\lambda,\qquad Q_{2i-1} = (2i-1)\lambda^2,\qquad 
S_{2i} = 2i\lambda,\qquad Q_{2i} = 2i\lambda^2 \le 2i .
\]  
Thus the constraints are satisfied for any \(k\).  

Now choose a positive integer \(k\) such that  
\[
\lambda^2 < \frac{2k+2}{2k+5}.
\]  
Such \(k\) exists because the right‑hand side tends to \(1\) as \(k\to\infty\) and \(\lambda^2<1\).  
On his next even turn (turn \(2k+2\)) Bazza chooses  
\[
a_{k+1} = \sqrt{2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2}.
\]  
We have \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\) because squaring gives  
\[
2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2 > 4\lambda^2
\iff 2k+2 > (2k+5)\lambda^2
\iff \lambda^2 < \frac{2k+2}{2k+5},
\]  
which holds by our choice of \(k\).  

After this move,  
\[
Q_{2k+2} = (2k+1)\lambda^2 + \bigl(2k+2 - (2k+1)\lambda^2\bigr) = 2k+2,
\]  
so Bazza’s constraint is satisfied.  
Alice’s next turn is at \(n=2k+3\). The sum before her move is  
\[
S_{2k+2} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k} + a_{k+1} = (2k-1)\lambda + \lambda + a_{k+1} = 2k\lambda + a_{k+1}.
\]  
Since \(a_{k+1} > 2\lambda\), we obtain  
\[
S_{2k+2} > 2k\lambda + 2\lambda = (2k+2)\lambda.
\]  
The linear condition for Alice at \(n=2k+3\) requires  
\[

\]

The game is complex, but we can use a "potential" argument. I recall a classic problem: Alice and Bazza choose numbers with constraints on sum and sum of squares. The critical value is λ=1. We can treat the game as a "resource" allocation: At each turn, the player can decide to spend his resource to increase the sum. The constraints are:

- For Alice: She must have S_n ≤ λ n. She wants to keep S_n as small as possible to avoid Bazza's win? Perhaps the key is that the outcome is determined by the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. If Alice plays to maximize S_n (by setting x_n = λ), then Q_{2k-1} ≥ (2k-1)λ^2. The problem likely has a known solution: Alice wins iff λ > 1, Bazza wins iff λ < 1, and at λ = 1 it's a draw. This is reminiscent of the strategy for the game where the linear resource is in the denominator. In fact, we can prove that for λ < 1, Bazza can win by a strategy similar to the one we used earlier: He chooses a_1 = λ, a_2 = λ, ..., a_k = λ for some k, then at his (k+1)th move, he chooses a_{k+1} = sqrt(2k+2 - (2k+1)λ^2) > 2λ. For λ<1, as k increases, this sqrt is about sqrt(2k - 2kλ^2) = sqrt(2k(1-λ^2)). So it grows like sqrt(k), while (k+3)λ grows linearly. So for large k, sqrt(k) is much smaller than k, so the condition fails. Thus, if Alice plays zero, Bazza cannot make a_{k+1} large enough to cause her loss after his move. So the strategy of playing λ for the first k turns and then a big number does not guarantee a win if Alice plays zero.

Thus, Bazza needs a more sophisticated strategy. The game might be that for λ<1, Bazza can win by making his moves as large as possible given the square constraint, and then using the fact that the sum of squares after his moves is exactly 2k, so that the square sum after Alice's turn is 2k + λ^2? Actually if he always uses full budget, then q_k = 2k, so Q_{2k} = 2k. Then Alice at turn 2k+1 can choose x_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ - S_{2k}. If she chooses the maximum, then S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ. Then the next Bazza turn, he must choose a_{k+1} such that Q_{2k+2} = Q_{2k+1} + a_{k+1}^2 ≤ 2k+2. But Q_{2k+1} = q_k + (2λ - a_k)^2 = 2k + (2λ - a_k)^2. So the radicand is 2k+2 - (2k + (2λ - a_k)^2) = 2 - (2λ - a_k)^2. So a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2). This is the recurrence we had. For λ<1, this recurrence gives a sequence a_k that converges to a* = λ + √(1-λ^2). Since a* is less than 2λ for λ>1/√2, the a_k will never exceed 2λ. So Bazza cannot win by using the maximal strategy. However, if λ<1/√2, then a* > 2λ, so eventually a_k > 2λ, and he wins. So for λ<1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy by using the maximal strategy (always taking the maximum allowed). For λ between 1/√2 and 1, the maximal strategy does not win. Could there be another strategy? Let's test λ=0.9 numerically. If Bazza uses the maximal strategy, we have a_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2)= sqrt(2-0.81)= sqrt(1.19)=1.091, a_2 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_1)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.091)^2)= sqrt(2-0.709^2)= sqrt(2-0.502)= sqrt(1.498)=1.224, a_3 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_2)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.224)^2)= sqrt(2-0.576^2)= sqrt(2-0.332)= sqrt(1.668)=1.291, a_4 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_3)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.291)^2)= sqrt(2-0.509^2)= sqrt(2-0.259)= sqrt(1.741)=1.319, a_5 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_4)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.319)^2)= sqrt(2-0.481^2)= sqrt(2-0.231)= sqrt(1.769)=1.330, a_6 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_5)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.330)^2)= sqrt(2-0.47^2)= sqrt(2-0.2209)= sqrt(1.7791)=1.334, a_7 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_6)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.334)^2)= sqrt(2-0.466^2)= sqrt(2-0.217)= sqrt(1.783)=1.335, a_8 = 1.3359, etc. So a_k tends to about 1.3359, which is less than 2λ=1.8. So no win.

Now, can Bazza deviate to win? For instance, he could choose a_1 smaller than the maximum to increase his deficit. Then later, the recurrence could produce larger a_k. Let's try to see if we can get a_k > 2λ at some step. The recurrence for the maximum is a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2). If we choose a_k smaller than the maximum, then the next radicand is 2 - (2λ - a_k)^2, which is larger because (2λ - a_k) is larger. So a_{k+1} becomes larger. So by choosing a_k smaller, we can get a larger a_{k+1}. However, the deficit d_k = 2k - q_k becomes positive. Then at the next step, the radicand is 2 - (2λ - a_k)^2 + d_k? Actually the recurrence for the maximum is derived under the assumption that the deficit is zero. If the deficit is positive, the radicand becomes 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2, which is larger. So by building up a positive deficit, we can make later a_k larger. So we need to find if there exists a sequence of a_i such that at some step, a_k > 2λ. Since we have control over a_i, we can try to make them as large as possible. The ultimate limit: the maximum possible a_k given that the square sum after his moves is at most 2k, and the cumulative square sum after Alice's moves is bounded. The process is similar to the one we had for λ<1, but we need to see if he can make a_k exceed 2λ. We can attempt to set a_1 = λ (which is less than the maximum 1.091). Then we have d_1 = 2-λ^2 - a_1^2 = 2-0.81-0.81=0.38? Actually 2-λ^2=2-0.81=1.19, a_1^2=0.81, so d_1=0.38. Then at his second move, the maximum a_2 = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Q_3 = q_2 + (2λ - a_1)^2. q_2 = λ^2 + a_1^2 = 0.81+0.81=1.62. (2λ - a_1)=1.8-0.9=0.9, square=0.81. So Q_3=1.62+0.81=2.43. Then a_2_max = sqrt(4-2.43)= sqrt(1.57)=1.253. That's less than 1.8. So not win. Then d_2 = d_1 + 2 - (2λ - a_1)^2 - a_2^2 = 0.38 + 2 - 0.81 - 1.57 = 0.38+2-2.38=0. So d_2=0. So the deficit returns to zero. Then a_3 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_2)^2) = sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.253)^2)= sqrt(2 - 0.547^2)= sqrt(2-0.299)= sqrt(1.701)=1.304. Still <1.8. So not win.

If we choose a_1 even smaller, say a_1=0.5, then d_1 = 2-0.81-0.25=0.94. Then a_2_max = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Q_3 = q_2 + (2λ - a_1)^2. q_2 = λ^2 + a_1^2 = 0.81+0.25=1.06. (2λ - a_1)=1.8-0.5=1.3, square=1.69. So Q_3=1.06+1.69=2.75. Then a_2_max = sqrt(4-2.75)= sqrt(1.25)=1.118. Then d_2 = d_1 + 2 - (2λ - a_1)^2 - a_2^2 = 0.94 + 2 - 1.69 - 1.25 = 0.94+2-2.94=0. So d_2=0. Then a_3 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_2)^2) = sqrt(2 - (1.8-1.118)^2)= sqrt(2 - 0.682^2)= sqrt(2-0.465)= sqrt(1.535)=1.239. Still <1.8. So not win.

Thus, the maximal a_k in the recurrence is bounded by the fixed point a* = λ+√(1-λ^2). For λ=0.9, a*=1.3359. So no matter what, a_k cannot exceed that. So Bazza cannot win for λ between 1/√2 and 1. So the only way he can win is if his moves can exceed 2λ. That happens if a* > 2λ, i.e., λ < 1/√2. So for λ < 1/√2, Bazza can win by using the maximal strategy. For λ between 1/√2 and 1, the maximal strategy does not win, and we suspect that no strategy can win. But we need to prove that for λ in (1/√2, 1), Bazza cannot force a win. We have already argued that if Alice plays the maximum allowed (x_n = λ), then Bazza's best response leads to a_k that never exceeds 2λ. But if Alice plays smaller, could Bazza cause her loss? Possibly, if Alice plays small, she reduces the sum, making it easier for Bazza to push the sum up? But wait, the condition for her to lose is that S_{2k+2} > (2k+3)λ. If she plays 0, then S_{2k+2} = sum_{i=1}^k a_i. To achieve this for some k, she needs the sum of a_i to be large. But the a_i are limited by the square budget. In fact, we can prove that for λ < 1, the sum of a_i for any feasible sequence is at most something like k a*? Actually we can derive an upper bound on the sum of a_i from the constraint Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. The square sum after his moves is q_k = sum_{i=1}^k a_i^2. By Cauchy-Schwarz, (sum a_i)^2 ≤ k q_k ≤ k * 2k = 2k^2, so sum a_i ≤ √2 k. Then the condition sum a_i > (2k+1)λ would require √2 k > (2k+1)λ, i.e., λ < √2 k/(2k+1) ≈ √2/2 = 0.7071. So if λ > 0.7071, this inequality cannot hold for large k. But for small k, it might be possible. For λ=0.9, we need sum a_i > 1.8k+0.9. The maximum possible sum a_i for a given k is achieved when the a_i are as large as possible, which is limited by the square budget. The maximum possible sum a_i under the constraint sum a_i^2 ≤ 2k is, by Cauchy-Schwarz, at most √(k * 2k) = √2 k. So sum a_i ≤ 1.414k. Then we need 1.414k > 1.8k+0.9 ⇒ -0.386k > 0.9 ⇒ impossible. So for any k, the sum of a_i cannot exceed 1.414k, which is less than 1.8k+0.9 for all k≥1. Thus, it's impossible for the sum to exceed (2k+1)λ. This suggests that Bazza cannot win for λ > 1/√2 ≈0.7071. However, this argument assumes that Alice plays 0, so that the sum after his even turn is sum a_i. But if she plays nonzero, the sum could be larger. However, we are considering optimal play: Alice will try to avoid losing, so she will choose her numbers to minimize the sum after Bazza's moves. The sum after her odd turn is S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1}. She can choose x_{2k+1} as small as possible, but she must also satisfy that S_{2k+1} ≤ (2k+1)λ. So she can set x_{2k+1}=0, then S_{2k+1}=S_{2k}. Then the sum after Bazza's next move is S_{2k+2} = S_{2k} + a_{k+1}. So the sum after his move is the sum of his previous a_i plus a_{k+1}. So the sum is cumulative. The condition for her loss at the next odd turn is that S_{2k+2} > λ (2k+3). If she chooses x_{2k+1}=0, then S_{2k+2} = S_{2k} + a_{k+1}. But S_{2k} itself is the sum of his previous a_i (since she always picks 0). So S_{2k} = sum_{i=1}^k a_i. Then the condition is sum_{i=1}^{k+1} a_i > (2k+3)λ. This is stronger. To achieve this, Bazza would need his cumulative sum to be large. But we already have an upper bound on the cumulative sum from the square budget: by Cauchy-Schwarz, sum_{i=1}^k a_i ≤ √(k * sum a_i^2) ≤ √(k * 2k) = √2 k. So the maximum cumulative sum after k moves is about 1.414k. For λ=0.9, we need 1.414k > 1.8k+0.9, which is impossible. So Bazza cannot win by making his moves as large as possible. But could he make his moves larger than the Cauchy-Schwarz bound? The Cauchy-Schwarz bound is tight when the a_i are all equal. But the square budget constraint is sum a_i^2 ≤ 2k. The maximum sum a_i for a fixed sum of squares is achieved by making all a_i equal, so sum a_i ≤ √(k * 2k) = √2 k. So indeed, the sum of a_i is at most √2 k. Thus, for any feasible sequence, the cumulative sum after k moves is ≤ √2 k. So for Bazza to cause Alice to lose, we need √2 k > (2k+1)λ for some k. This inequality is equivalent to λ < √2 k/(2k+1). For λ > √2/2 = 1/√2 ≈0.7071, the right side is less than λ? Actually √2 k/(2k+1) = √2/(2+1/k) which tends to √2/2 = 0.7071 from above. So for λ > 0.7071, there exists no k such that λ < √2 k/(2k+1) because the maximum of the right side is for k=1? For k=1, √2/3≈0.4714; for k=2, √2/2.5≈0.5657; for k=3, √2/2.333≈0.606; for k=4, √2/2.25≈0.628; for k=5, √2/2.2≈0.646; for k=6, √2/2.1667≈0.653; as k→∞, it approaches 0.7071. So the right side is always ≤ 0.7071. Thus, if λ > 0.7071, the inequality λ < √2 k/(2k+1) can never hold. Therefore, for λ > 1/√2, the cumulative sum after any number of moves cannot exceed (2k+1)λ. So Bazza cannot force Alice to lose, regardless of her choices? But we must consider that Alice might not set her numbers to 0; she could set them larger, which would increase the sum before Bazza's move, making it easier for him to cause her loss. However, she wants to survive, so she will set them as small as possible. So her optimal strategy is to always pick 0. Then as argued, Bazza's cumulative sum is bounded by √2 k, and for λ > 1/√2, we have √2 k < (2k+1)λ for all k. Therefore, Bazza cannot win. So for λ in (1/√2, 1), the game can go on forever with Alice picking 0 and Bazza picking some numbers? But Bazza's moves must also satisfy Q_n ≤ n. If he picks numbers that are not too large, he can continue. For λ=0.9, we can see if there is a feasible infinite play. Then Alice picks 0 each time, and the game continues indefinitely. So indeed, for λ=0.9, there is a draw (infinite play). So Bazza does not have a winning strategy.

Thus, the classification is:

- For λ < 1/√2, Bazza can win (by using the maximal strategy, where his moves converge to a* > 2λ, and eventually a_k > 2λ, causing Alice's loss).
- For λ = 1/√2, Bazza cannot win by the maximal strategy because a* = 2λ, and then the condition a_k > 2λ never holds. But can he win by another strategy? Let's test λ=1/√2≈0.70710678. If he uses the maximal strategy, a_k → 1.4142, and a_k never exceeds 2λ=1.4142. However, could he use a different strategy to make a_k > 2λ? The maximum possible a_k given the budget is a* = λ+√(1-λ^2) = 0.7071+0.7071=1.4142. So he cannot exceed that. So he cannot win at any step. But could he win at a later step by using a deficit? If he builds up a deficit, then the radicand at the next step is 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2. To get a_{k+1} > 2λ, we need 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2 > 4λ^2. Since λ^2 = 0.5, 4λ^2 = 2. So we need 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2 > 2 ⇒ d_k > (2λ - a_k)^2. If a_k is small, (2λ - a_k)^2 is large, so d_k must be large. But can d_k be large? d_k = 2k - q_k. q_k is the square sum after his moves. If he chooses his a_i to be small, then q_k is small, so d_k is large. However, the radicand at step k+1 is 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2. If a_k is small, this radicand could be > 4λ^2? Let's try to see if Bazza can make a_{k+1} > 2λ for some k. Since the maximum possible a_k is 1.4142, and 2λ=1.4142, the condition a_{k+1} > 1.4142 would require that the radicand > 2, i.e., 2 + d_k - (2λ - a_k)^2 > 2 ⇒ d_k > (2λ - a_k)^2. For a_k = 0, we need d_k > 4λ^2 = 2. So if he can build up a deficit d_k > 2, then he can set a_{k+1} > 2λ. But can he build up a deficit? d_k = 2k - q_k. To have d_k > 2, we need q_k < 2k - 2. Since q_k = sum a_i^2, and a_i are nonnegative, he can choose his a_i to be very small, so that q_k is small. For example, if he sets a_1 = 0.1, then q_1=0.01, d_1=2-0.01=1.99. Then a_2_max = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Q_3 = q_2 + (2λ - a_1)^2. q_2 = λ^2 + a_1^2 = 0.5+0.01=0.51. (2λ - a_1)=1.4142-0.1=1.3142, square=1.727, Q_3=0.51+1.727=2.237. Then a_2_max = sqrt(4-2.237)= sqrt(1.763)=1.328. Then d_2 = d_1 + 2 - (2λ - a_1)^2 - a_2^2 = 1.99 + 2 - 1.727 - 1.763 = 1.99+2-3.49=0.5. So d_2 is not large. In fact, it seems that the deficit cannot stay large because the term (2λ - a_k)^2 uses up the budget. To build a large deficit, we need to have q_k small, but then the next step will have a large (2λ - a_k)^2, which will reduce the radicand. The process is self-correcting. The fixed point analysis with d=0 gives the supremum of a_k. For λ=1/√2, the maximum a_k is exactly 2λ. So no matter what, a_k cannot exceed 2λ. Therefore, Bazza cannot win for λ=1/√2.

Now, for λ between 1/√2 and 1, we already argued that Bazza cannot win because the maximum possible sum of a_i is ≤ √2 k, and for λ > 1/√2, we have √2 k < (2k+1)λ for all k. Thus, even if Alice picks 0, Bazza's cumulative sum cannot exceed (2k+1)λ, so he cannot cause her loss. So Bazza does not have a winning strategy. For λ=1, this also holds (√2 k < 2k+1 for all k). So at λ=1, it's a draw.

Now, what about λ > 1? We already proved that Alice can win by playing λ each time. So the set where Alice wins is λ > 1.

Now, we must also consider λ in (0, 1/√2). In this region, Bazza can win by using the maximal strategy. Let's prove that for λ < 1/√2, the recurrence a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2) (assuming he uses full budget) produces a sequence that eventually exceeds 2λ. Since a_k increases to a* = λ + √(1-λ^2), and for λ < 1/√2, we have a* > 2λ. Moreover, the sequence is increasing and converges to a* from below. Therefore, there exists some K such that a_K > 2λ. Then after Alice's turn at odd n=2K-1 (assuming she plays max), S_{2K-1} = (2K-1)λ, and Bazza's next move at n=2K can be set to a_K (which is >2λ), making S_{2K} = (2K-1)λ + a_K > (2K-1)λ + 2λ = (2K+1)λ, so Alice loses at her next turn. However, this requires that Alice plays the maximum allowed at each odd turn. If she does not, could she survive? She wants to survive, so she will try to keep the sum small. But we need to show that regardless of her choices, Bazza can force a win. We can use the potential argument: Bazza can adopt the strategy of always taking the maximum possible x_n given the square budget. This is a well-defined strategy. Then we analyze the process regardless of Alice's choices. We need to show that eventually, after some step, the sum S_n will be > λ n for Alice's next turn. This is the typical "game of quadratic resource". Let's define the process:

Let the game be played with Bazza using the maximal strategy: at each even turn n=2k, he chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1} - (2λ - a_{k-1})^2) (the maximum allowed). Then we have a recurrence for the quantities after his moves.

We can try to prove that for λ < 1/√2, after some finite number of moves, the sum S_{2k} will exceed λ (2k+1). But this depends on Alice's choices. However, we can also prove that if Alice chooses her numbers as small as possible (0), then the sum after his moves is sum a_i, and we have an upper bound on sum a_i from the square budget. For λ < 1/√2, we can also find a k such that √2 k > (2k+1)λ? Actually for λ < 1/√2, we have √2 k/(2k+1) > λ? For k=1, √2/3 ≈0.4714; for λ=0.5, 0.5 >0.4714? Actually 0.5 >0.4714, so not. For k=2, √2/2.5≈0.5657; for λ=0.5, 0.5<0.5657, so condition holds for k=2? We need λ < √2 k/(2k+1) for some k. For λ=0.5, at k=2, 0.5 < 0.5657, so yes, there exists k. But this inequality gives the possibility that the sum of a_i could exceed (2k+1)λ? The maximum sum is √2 k, which is about 1.414k. For k=2, 1.414*2=2.828, and (2*2+1)λ=5*0.5=2.5, so 2.828 > 2.5. So it's possible that the sum of a_i exceeds 2.5, so Bazza could win if Alice plays 0. But is that feasible? With λ=0.5, the maximal strategy gives a_1 = sqrt(2-λ^2)= sqrt(2-0.25)=1.3229, a_2 = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_1)^2)= sqrt(2 - (1-1.3229)^2)= sqrt(2 - 0.3229^2)= sqrt(2-0.1043)= sqrt(1.8957)=1.376, sum=2.6989, which is >2.5. So indeed, if Alice plays 0, Bazza can win at turn 4? Let's simulate: λ=0.5. Alice: x_1=0, S1=0. Bazza: a_1 = 1.3229 (max), S2=1.3229, Q2=1.75. Alice: x_3 = 0, S3=1.3229, Q3=1.75. Bazza: a_2 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4-1.75)= sqrt(2.25)=1.5, S4=2.8229, Q4=1.75+2.25=4.0. Alice: need S4 ≤ 3? Actually 3λ=1.5, S4=2.8229>1.5, so she cannot move. So Bazza wins. So the maximal strategy works.

Thus, for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy by always taking the maximum allowed. For λ in (1/√2, 1), no matter what Bazza does, if Alice plays optimally (always 0), the cumulative sum after his moves is ≤ √2 k, which is less than (2k+1)λ, so he cannot cause her loss. However, could he cause her loss if she plays nonzero? She will play 0 to avoid loss. So Bazza cannot win. So for λ in (1/√2, 1), the game can go on forever with Alice playing 0 and Bazza playing, say, λ each turn. So it's a draw. For λ=1, it's also a draw (Alice can play 0, Bazza plays 1). At λ=1/√2, it's also a draw.

Thus, the classification is:

- Bazza wins iff 0 < λ < 1/√2.
- Alice wins iff λ > 1.
- For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, neither wins (draw).

But we need to double-check the lower bound for Bazza's win: Is it λ < 1/√2? What about λ very close to 0? Yes. For λ=0.1, we can check: a_1 = sqrt(2-0.01)= sqrt(1.99)=1.410, 2λ=0.2, so a_1 > 0.2. So he wins at turn 2. So that's fine.

Thus, the answer: Alice has a winning strategy for λ > 1; Bazza has a winning strategy for 0 < λ < 1/√2. At λ = 1/√2 and λ = 1, neither has a winning strategy (the game can go on forever). The problem asks: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So we should state:

Alice wins if and only if λ > 1.
Bazza wins if and only if 0 < λ < 1/√2.

We can also note that at λ = 1/√2 and λ = 1, the game can be forced to go on forever, so neither player has a winning strategy.

Now, we must produce a rigorous proof. The proof we wrote above for Alice's win is rigorous. For Bazza's win, we need to show that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza can adopt a strategy to win. We need to show that the maximal strategy (always taking the maximum allowed) works. We'll define the strategy and prove by induction that after each of his moves, the square sum is exactly 2k (i.e., he uses the full budget), and that the sequence a_k is increasing and converges to a* > 2λ, so eventually a_k > 2λ. We also need to handle Alice's possible choices. To show that the strategy works regardless of Alice's moves, we can use the fact that he chooses his numbers based on the current state, and we can prove that if Alice plays any legal moves, then after some finite number of steps, the sum S_n will exceed λ n. A standard way: Consider the function F_n = nλ^2 - Q_n. For λ<1/√2, we can show that eventually F_n becomes negative? Not sure.

But we can use the following approach: Define the process where Bazza always chooses the maximum allowed. We can prove that this strategy ensures that after each of his moves, the quantity q_k = Q_{2k} = 2k (full budget). Then the next Alice move (if she chooses the maximum) will cause S_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ. Then the next Bazza move a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2). This recurrence yields a_k increasing to a* > 2λ. Hence eventually a_k > 2λ, and then Alice cannot move. But what if Alice does not choose the maximum? She could choose smaller numbers. Then the sum S_{2k+1} is smaller, which might make it harder for Bazza to push her sum up. However, we can argue that if Alice chooses smaller numbers, the square sum after her turn is even smaller, which actually gives Bazza a larger radicand at his next move. Because his radicand is 2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}. And Q_{2k+1} = q_k + (2λ - a_k)^2 (since she chooses x_{2k+1} = (2k+1)λ - S_{2k}, but if she chooses smaller, S_{2k+1} is smaller, so (2λ - a_k) is not directly the increment; the increment in the square sum from her move is x_{2k+1}^2. If she chooses a smaller x, then Q_{2k+1} is smaller, so the radicand is larger, which allows Bazza to choose a larger a_{k+1}. So by playing smaller, Alice actually helps Bazza! So the worst-case for Bazza is when Alice plays the maximum, making the sum as large as possible, which gives him the smallest radicand. So if Bazza can win when Alice plays max, he can certainly win when she plays smaller. Therefore, it suffices to show that the strategy works against the "maximal" Alice. So we can assume Alice always plays the maximum allowed. Then the process reduces to the recurrence. So for λ < 1/√2, Bazza can win.

Thus, the rigorous proof:

1. Alice wins for λ > 1: as shown.
2. Bazza wins for λ < 1/√2: define his strategy as follows: on each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible x_n such that the sum of squares condition holds. This is always possible because the budget is linear. We prove by induction that after his k-th move, the sum of squares is exactly 2k, and that the sequence a_k (his chosen numbers) satisfies a_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - a_k)^2) with a_1 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then we show that a_k is increasing and converges to a* = λ + √(1-λ^2). Since λ < 1/√2, we have a* > 2λ. Therefore, there exists an index K such that a_K > 2λ. Then after Alice's turn at n=2K-1 (if she plays the maximum), we have S_{2K-1} = (2K-1)λ. Bazza's next move (turn 2K) is a_K, which is >2λ, so S_{2K} = (2K-1)λ + a_K > (2K-1)λ + 2λ = (2K+1)λ. Then Alice cannot make a legal move at n=2K+1 because she would need S_{2K+1} ≤ (2K+1)λ, but S_{2K} already exceeds that. So Bazza wins. If Alice does not play the maximum, then the sum after her turn is even smaller, which only makes it easier for Bazza to cause her loss (since the radicand for his move becomes larger). Hence the strategy works.

3. For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, we show that neither player can force a win. Alice can ensure infinite play by always picking x_n = 0. Then Bazza can also play x_n = λ (or any number that keeps the square sum within budget). Since λ^2 ≤ 1, the square sum after his moves is kλ^2 ≤ 2k, so his moves are legal. Thus the game continues indefinitely. Hence no winning strategy exists for either player.

We also need to consider λ=1/√2: the strategy for Bazza using maximal moves does not win because a_k never exceeds 2λ. But could he use a different strategy to win? For λ=1/√2, the maximum possible a_k is 2λ, so he cannot exceed that. Thus no winning strategy. So draw.

Thus, the answer: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bazza wins for 0 < λ < 1/√2.

We analyze the game with parameter \(\lambda>0\).  
Turn \(n\):

- if \(n\) is odd, Alice chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(\sum_{i=1}^{n}x_i\le \lambda n\);
- if \(n\) is even, Bazza chooses \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(\sum_{i=1}^{n}x_i^2\le n\).

If a player cannot make a legal move, the other wins.  
We determine for which \(\lambda\) Alice has a winning strategy and for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

**Alice’s winning strategy for \(\lambda>1\).**  
Alice plays the maximum allowed at every odd turn: \(x_{2k-1}=\lambda\) (the sum condition becomes equality).  
After her \(k\)-th turn we have \(S_{2k-1}=(2k-1)\lambda\).  
By Cauchy–Schwarz,  
\[
S_{2k-1}^2\le (2k-1)Q_{2k-1}\quad\text{where } Q_{2k-1}=\sum_{i=1}^{2k-1}x_i^2,
\]  
hence \(Q_{2k-1}\ge S_{2k-1}^2/(2k-1)=(2k-1)\lambda^2\).  
Because \(\lambda^2>1\), the quantity \((2k-1)\lambda^2\) grows faster than \(2k\).  
There exists an integer \(k_0\) such that \((2k_0-1)\lambda^2>2k_0\) (e.g. take \(k_0\) large enough).
